made\non limits\nfor continuous\ncontaminants\nother than those\nsuggested\nby the\n\nEVALUATION\n\nOF\n\nTHE\n\nCONTAMINANT\n\nPROBLEM\n\nToxicological\nproblems\nin space\noperations\ncover\nthree\nsituations:\n{I} the\nacute,\nshort term,\nhigh-level\nexposure\neither in ground\nsupport\nor space\ncabin\nconditions;\n(2) the 8-hour\nwork\nday exposure\nfound in manufacturing\nand ground\nsupport\nsituations;\nand (3) continuous,\nlong term\nexposure\nto trace contaminants,\nsuch as would\nbe anticipated\nin extended\nspace\nmissions.\nA completely\nnew and unique\naspect\nof toxicology\nhas evolved\nfrom\nthe\naerospace\nmission,\nfor which\nno parallel\nexists in previous\nexperience\nin\nindustrial\nhygiene\nor occupational\nmedicine.\nThis situation\nexists for two\nreasons:\nthe increased\nuse of multi-ton\nquantities\nof high energy,\nphysiologically-reactive\ncompounds\nwith the inherent\nincreased\npossibility\nof accidental\nexposure;\nand the contemplated\nlong-term\nspace\nmission\nwithin a closed\nsystem,\nin which,\nunlike\nsubmarine\nconditions,\nunlimited\npower\nis not\navailable\nfor complete\ncontrol\nof the environmental\natmosphere.\nThe greatest\nneed\nfor toxicological\ninformation\nin both situations\nis for inhalation\ndata.\nUp to the present,\nthe only guidelines\navailable\nto the toxicologist\nare the\nThreshold\nLimit\nValues\n(TLV)\nestablished\nby the American\nConference\non\nGovernmental\nand\nIndustrial\nHygienists\n( 6,\n199\n), the\nmaximal\nallowable\nconcentrations\nof the\nAmerican\nStandards\nAssociationls\nZ-37\nCommittee\n(210),\nand\nthe\nmore\nrecent\nrecommendations\nmade\nby the\nad hoc\ncommittee\non Air\nStandards\nof the\nCommittee\non\nLife\nSciences,\nNational\nAcademy\nof Sciences\n{136}.\nThese\nvalues\nrepresent,\nfor the most\npart, time-weighted\naverage\nconcentrations\nto which\nnearly\nall workers\ncan be exposed\n8 hours\ndaily,\n5\ndays\nper week\nthroughout\ntheir working\nlifetime\nwithout\nadverse\neffects on\nhealth.\nSafe\nmilitary\n\nThe\n\nlast of these\n\nexposure\nsituations\n\nguidelines\n\nextends\n\nfew\n\nTLV\n\ndata\n\nto space\n\nconditions.\n\nlevels to air pollutants\nin community,\nindustrial\nand\nare based\nmainly\non industrial\nexperiences,\nanimal\n\nstudies,\n\nhuman\nvolunteer\nexposures\nor a combination\nof the three.\nBecause\nit is rare\nthat industrial\nexperience\ncan supply\nboth environmental\nand medical\ndata\nadequately\ncontrolled\nin respect\nto exposure\nconditions,\ndata from\nanimal\nstudies\nare more\ncommonly\nused\nwhere\nsuch controls\ncan be maintained\nand\nthe progress\nof the response\ndetermined\nat will.\nIncreasing\nuse of data from\nhuman\nvolunteer\nexposures\nis being made\nto provide\nthe desired\ncorrelations\n13-i\n\nbetween animal and human experiences.\nData from whatever source is then\nevaluated by a committee of individuals of long and continuing experience in\nindustrial health for what limit in their judgment is appropriate.\nThe continual surveillance on the suitability of the industrial air limits, which now\nnumber more than 400 and a substantial number (67\xc2\xb0_0) f which have been\no\nsatisfactorily used without change for from 20 to Z5 years, offer mute\ntestimony to their validity.\nIn this connection, it is interesting to note that\nexperience has been equally good with industrial air limits based on animal\nstudies as those based on human experience and industrial surveys (65%).(198)\nEach limit is documented by pertinent literature references with a clear\nstatement on the nature of the response against which the limit protects.\nIt should be most emphatically noted that in our present state of knowledge\nnone of the industrial air limits can be used with c,_.rtainty either directly or\nby extrapolation for space cabin environments.\nAlthough such an extrapolating\nequation has been proposed (ZOO)in which all variables likely to affect toxicity\nwere included, subsequent experimental animal work (205) showed that such\na procedure could not be relied on in any given ca_e; in our present state of\nknowledge, the rate of metabolism varied unpredictably from one pollutant to\nanother under conditions of continuous exposure relative to intermittent\nexposure. This parameter appeared to be overriding, at least as far as\nanimal studies indicated, but it should be noted that animal studies are incapable of revealing the magnitude of several of the factors included in the\nextrapolation equation. How unpredictable is the application of the TLVs of\nindustry to space cabin conditions is shown in the above-mentioned studies\n(Z05). The intermittent:, 8-hour industrial TLV for phenol appeared to be a\nsatisfactory TLV for the 90-day continuous TLV for space; possibly also for\ncarbon tetrachloride,\nbut not for hydrazine, unsxrm, dimethyl hydrazine,\nnitrogen dioxide, decaborane, hydrogen sulfide or methyl mercaptan.\nThe industrial air limits would appear to be also inadequate for extrapolation to the space cabin environment in terms :_I ,._ther critical factors of the\nenvironment\nsuch as pressure,\natmosphere,\nrelative humidity,\nradiation,\nthermal,\nand other factors.\nEven\nthe 90-day\nexposure\nlimits\nset for submarines\nare not directly\napplicable\nbecause\nof these\nvariables\n( 27,\n151,\n200).\nEfforts\nto use these values\nwhen\nmixtures\nof toxic materials\nare involved,\nas is almost\nalways\nthe case in aerospact\n_ situations,\nare not only\nmeaningless\n\nbut dangerous.\n\nIn view\nof the necessity\nfor provisional\nlin_ils of manned\nspace\nflights of\n90- to 1000-days\'\nduration,\nthe NAS-NRC\ncommittee\nhas derived\nthe following\ncriteria\nfor trace contaminant\ncontrol\nin manned\nspacecraft\n(136):\ni)\n\nContaminants\nthe\n\n2)\n\nmust\n\nphysiological,\n\nThe\nspacecraft\nmance\ndecrement\n\nnot\n\nproduce\n\nbiochemical,\n\nsignifica_t\n\nadverse\n\nchanges\n\nor\n\nstability\n\nof the\n\nmtxntal\n\nenvironment\nmust\nnot contribute\nof the crew\nthat will _,ndanger\n\nin\ncrew.\n\nto a performission\n\nobjectives.\n3)\n\n13-Z\n\nThe\nspacecraft\nenvironment\nor biological\nexperiments\n\nmust\nnot interfere\nwith physical\nnor with medical\nmonitoring.\n\nFor the purposes of these provisional criteria, the Committee assumes\na spacecraft atmosphere ranging from 760 to 258 mm Hg total pressure, containing nitrogen as a diluent gas, oxygen sufficient to maintain normal (sealevel equivalent) alveolar partial pressure, and carbon dioxide below 5 mm Hg.\nTemperature and relative humidity are within the comfort zone for the total\npressure selected.\nThis rigorous approach is also consistent with scientiflc requirements.\nThe NASA Space Medicine Advisory Group and the Respiratory Physiology\nGroup of the Space Science Board\'s 1966 Summer Study have reaffirmed the\nprinciple that engineering exigencies should not dictate the environment: the\nenvironment must be supplied to provide the best medium for the experimental\neffort and one might also add, the best medium for the mission profile.\nThus,\nif one of the goals of prolonged manned spaceflight is to ascertain man\'s\nadaptability and response to the weightless environment, it is necessary to\ndesign manned spacecraft in such a fashion that the Earth atmosphere or a\nreasonable facsimile thereof be provided in order not to prejudice the study\nof the one facet of spaceflight that cannot be duplicated on Earth - weightlessness.\nThe NAS-NRC has therefore developed conservative air quality standards\nfor prolonged manned missions on the following premises:\n(136)\nl)\n\nAny contamination\n\nof the spacecraft atmosphere\n\nmay\n\nbe\n\ndetri-\n\nmental.\n2)\n\nZero\ncontamination\nimpossible.\n\nlevel\n\n3}\n\nData do not exist that will permit\none to predict\nwith certainty\nthe multiple\n\nThe uncertainties\nin establishing\neven provisional\nlimits for prolonged\nmanned\nmissions\nare many\nand range\nfrom\nengineeringp\nto environmental,\nto\ntoxicological\nconsiderations.\nSince the materials\nto be used\nin future\nspacecraft construction\nand the type of regenerative\nenvironmental\ncontrol\nsystem{s)\nto be For all these\nreasons, the limits recommended by the NAS-NRC Committee are provisional,\nand subject to revision.\n(See discussion of Table 13-15 on page 13-56.)\nPrograms have been established to provide specific toxicological information on selected propellants and to study the effects of long term, continuous\nexposure to possible trace contaminants at reduced atmospheric pressures\nand under the influence of one- and two-gas systems (oxygen or oxygen/\nnitrogen) (2, 136, Z03 ). These programs include definitive measurements\nof physiological changes as evidenced by clinical chemistry, changes in\nbehavioral patterns, and gross and microscopic pathology. stated: (204)\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Given a certain cabin volume, the time of equilibration of contaminant concentrations in the atmosphere is independent of the\nfinal concentration attained, if contaminant generation and removal rates are held constant. Under certain conditions, the\nvirtual (but not the true) rates of contaminant generation can\nbecome constant since the decreasing rate of gas -off from\nmaterials is balanced out by the progressiw_ loss of filtering\nefficiency.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 The magnitude of the final equilibrium concentration of contaminants is directly proportional to the generation rate and inversely\nproportional to the removal rate in sealed cabins.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Contaminant concentration rises rapidly at first and then approaches\na constant value (equilibrium concentration) at infinite time.\nFrom a practical standpoint, the design engineer has primarily to worry\nabout contaminant removal rates to keep the atmosphere habitable on long\nduration missions.\nRemoval rates depend on what contaminants are lost\ntogether with the cabin atmosphere as the result of outboard leak and the\namount of contaminants that are adsorbed on the various filter beds. Leak\nrates are the most effective disposal methods for contaminants.\nThey also\nhave the greatest impact on the rate at which contaminants accumulate in the\ncabin (230). Since large leak rates are undesirable from a logistics standpoint, other means of contaminant elimination must be found. To simplify\ncalculations, "equivalent leak-rate times" can be used for rating filters,\nscrubbers or other air purification equipment. This "equivalent leak-rate\ntime" (ELRT) can be defined as the volume of atmosphere that has been\n"absolutely cleaned of contaminants" in one day\'s time, with specific consideration for the efficiency of the purification unit. For example: A filter\noperating at 50% efficiency would remove only I/2 of the contaminants present\nin a unit-volume of air passing through it. On the, second passage through, it\nwould remove i/2 of the remainder, etc.\nIn evaluating the buildup rate, important secondary factors to be considered for each contaminant are the kinetics of adsorption along adsorption\nbeds and the break-through curves for the gas-bed system (33, 157 ). The\nproblem with filters is that their efficiency decreases with time as the filter\nbed saturates with contaminants and as the flow rates through the filter drop\ndue to particles obliterating the free passage of atmosphere.\nTypical curves\nare seen in Figure 13-Z. These curves also determine the nature and timing\nof secondary chemical reactions which can occur on the bed and thus the\nalteration in the nature of the trace contaminants to be considered.\nIt can be\nseen that with increasing time, filter efficiency decreases in an exponential\nfashion that is quite similar to the build-up of contaminants.\nConsequently,\nthe inefficiencies of filters on long-duration missions will be greatly aggravated by the contaminant build-up if there is no substantial outboard leak or\ncontrolled dumping. Moreover, nuclear submarine experience indicates\nthat adsorbent beds become saturated with high boiling hydrocarbons within\ntwo to three days and permit the low boiling point materials to pass through,\n13-8\n\n--\n\nBED\n\nSATURATION\n\n.\nFigure\nConcentration\nDLSfA@IC_\nCONTA2,;I:IA:IT\n\n0\n\nALOiIG\n\n_,_D\n\nCOrJCENTRATIO,\'I\n\nCONCENTRATION\n\nProfiLes in Sorption\n\nBeds\n\nL\nt-.LCrlG\n\nTHE\n\nOEO\n\nIN\n\nLEAVIN3\n\nTIME\nCONTAMINANT\n\n13-2\n\n(After\n\nThomas (203))\n\nt"\nGAS\n\nBEO\n\neven displacing\nthese.\nUntil specific\nadsorption\ncoefficients\nbecome\navailable\nfor particular\nmaterials\nand periods\nof time,\ngenerous\nsafety factors\nshould\nbe used\nin calculating\nthe amount\nof adsorbents.\nPreliminary\ndesign\ndata are\navailable\non isotope-heated,\ncatalytic\noxidizing\nsystems\nfor contaminant\nremoval\nin future\nspace\nvehicles\n(142).\nGeneration\nof contaminants\ncan be controlled\nby appropriate\ncare\nin the\ndesign\nand manufacture\nof space\ncabins.\nA handbook\nis available\ncovering\nrecommendations\nfor contaminant\ncontrol\nin the Apollo\nProgram\n(138).\nThe\nSoviet\napproach\nto the kinetics\nof contaminant\ncontrol\nhas been\nrecorded\n(24).\nTOXICOLOGICAL\n\nFACTORS\n\nAll compounds\ncapable\nof chemical\nreaction\nhave an adverse\neffect on\nthe body\nat some\nquantity\nor concentration\n(50, 52, 145, 177, 218\n). Toxicity\nis inherent\nin all substances;\nthe question,\ntherefore,\nis: what is the intensity\nand duration\nof the exposure?\nBy the process\nof homeostasis\nthrough\nalteration of physiological\nand biochemical\nmechanisms,\nthe body\nis capable\nof\nmaintaining\nits normal\nhealthy\ninternal\nenvironment\nup to a point.\nIf that\npoint is exceeded,\nthen adverse\neffects may\noccur.\nExposure\nto chemical\nsubstances\nmay\nbe single,\nrepeated,\nor continuous.\nThe vast majority\nof\ntoxicologic\nresearch\nhas been based\non the first two of these\nrelationships.\nAs dose.\n\nSecondary\n\nFactors\n\nModifying\n\nToxic\n\nAction\n\nA number\nof factors\nrelated\nto the external\nenvironment\nor the peculiarities of the test species\nmodify\nthe extent and threshold\nfor toxic action.\nIntrinsic\n\nFactors\n\nThe\ngreatest\nsingle intrinsic\nfactor\ninfluencing\nthe response\nto a toxicant\nis the differences\namong\nspecies,\nalthough\na -14\n\nstate.\n\nNutrition\n\nplays\n\nan\n\nimportant\n\nrol_, and\n\nspecific\n\ndietary\n\ndeficiencies,\n\nmay modify susceptibility.\nTolerance developed to a particular agent may\nbring about resistance to progressively larger amounts of the agent.\nExtrinsic\n\nFactors\n\nThe number and spacing of the doses, time of exposure, particle size and\nsurface area of particulates,\ntogether with the total quantity administered,\nare the most important factors.\nThe route of administration\nsometimes\nalters the response, as does the physical state of the substance and its degree\nof chemical reactivity as determined by solubility.\nEnvironment conditions\nsuch as state of nutrition temperature, pressure, and the presence of other\nchemical substances are important modifying facturs, especially in the space\ncabin environment.\nTOXICOLOGY IN THE SPACECRAFT\n\nENVIRONMENT\n\nIn the context of cabin exposure, one is dealing with a toxicological\nproblem involving the prediction of probable responses to a low level, continuous exposure to a mixture of chemicals, some of which are not as yet\nknown. There is a constantly changing environment with each compound\nbuilding up to equilibrium levels in variable periods of time. Programmed\nor accidental cabin depressurizations\ncan cause sudden purges and reduction\nof concentration level to zero allowing excretion of the compound; then follows\nrehuildup in the body to a new equilibrium.\nThere is simultaneous adaptation\nor desensitization and variable levels of cross tolerance between compounds\n(170). Cumulative effects under such a variable exposure background are\nmost difficult to assess.\nIn order to set exposure limits in space operations, one must convert the\nusual industrial TLV values (TLVind) for 8 hrs/day, 5 days per week exposure to TLV values for continuous exposure in space (TZVspace).\nExperience\nwith submarines has required extrapolation of industrial TL-V data to 90 days\ncontinuous exposure (27, 151, 155, 187). At the industrial threshold limit\nand under normal conditions, continuous toxicity tests on animals for 90\ndays have shown evidence of a wide variation in safety factors of present\nthreshold limits.\nThese tests showed effects ranging from no mortality or\nother untoward effects, to moderate toxicity, to almost complete lethality in\nanimals tested over 90 days at the threshold limit.\nIt should be noted that\ntaking animal responses to continuous exposure as a measure of the safety\nfactor magnitude for intermittent exposure may not be entirely correct in all\ninstances; all substances are not cumulative either in amount or in effect on\ncontinuous exposure. The technique does provide a rough estimate of the\nsafety factor.\nThe following\n\nequation has been proposed:\n\n(ZOO)\n\nT LVin d x F\n=\nTLVspace\n\npress\nFcont\n\nx\n\nFtemp\n\nx F rm\n\nx FOz\n\n(12)\nx Flat\n\nx\n\nF_nt\n\n13 -15\n\nwhere F\n\nFcont\n\n= Dosage factor from ambient pressure change to\n5 psi = 3\n= Toxicity factor from continuous dosage = l to 4\n\nFtemp\nF rm\n\n= Toxicity\n= Toxicity\n\nfactor from temperature\n\nFO2\n\n= Toxicity\n\ndue to 100% O2 at 5 psi\n\nFfat\n\n= Toxicity\n\nfactor due to fatigue,\n\nF_nt\n\n= Toxicity factor from interaction\nfl x f2 x f3 "\'\'fn\n\npress\n\nfacto_ due to restricted\n\nchange, FAT\nmotion,\n\nF_M\n\nF AF\nof the factors =\n\nand where\nfl\nfz\n\n= toxicity from combination of 02 toxicity\nsubstance\n\nf3\nSince\n\n= toxicity above that from intermittent\nand that from 3-fold the dose\n\n=\n\nthe\n\ntoxicity\n\nspace\n\ncabin\n\nfrom\n\neffect\n\natmosphere,\n\nof 0 2 on\nin the\n\n8-hr exposure\nand toxic\n\nfatigue\nnear\n\nfuture\n\nat least,\n\nwill range\n\nfrom\n5 to 7 psia, the Fpres s = 3 appears\nadequate\nto cover\nthe fact that lung\nabsorption\nof gases\nis By simple\ndiffusion\nand accordingly\nis directly\nproportional to external\npressure\n(163).\nAs mentioned\nabove,\nthe multiplication\nof\n8 hr/day\ndata for 5 day week\nby 3 does\nnot give adequate\nsafety in all cases\nfor 24 hr/day\ncontinuous\nexposure.\nThe elusive\nmetabolic\ninteractions\ndetermining\nthis exact multiplication\nfactor have been discussed\nabove\nand\nelsewhere\n(71,\n200\nrise in temperature\n\n). The usual\nthe metabolic\n\nQl0 factor indicates\nthat for every\n10\xc2\xb0C\nrate of a cell will increase\nat least 2-fold.\n\nAlthough\nin general,\ntoxicity increases\nroughly\nby this factor as body temperature\nincreases,\nit is by no means\na universal\nrule (i00).\nThe restriction\nof movement\nand fatigue may\nadd further\nstressful\nconditions\nto the environment\nand alter to an as yet unknown\ndegree,\nthe response\nto some\ntoxic\nagents\nin humans\n(144, 200 ).\nThe\n\neffects\n\nof 5 psia,\n\non those\nagents\nwhich\nReduction\nof tocopherol\nalong\nwith a hemolytic\n(159).\n(See Nutrition,\n\nI00%\n\noxygen\n\nmay\n\nhave\n\na profound\n\neffect,\n\nespecially\n\ncan inactivate\nthe anti-oxidant\ndefenses\n(2, 160,\n161\n).\nin the plasma\nof Gemini\nastronauts\nhas been\nreported\nprocess\nwhich\nmay\nresemble\nthe anemia\nof acanthocytosis\nNo.\n14, and Oxygen-CO2-Energy,\nNo.\n10.)\n\nThere\nis some\nindication\nin animals\nthat oxyg_;n at 5 psia will synergize\nwith systemic\ntoxic agents\nsuch as CCL 4 (122, 194, 203 ). standard\n\nequivalent)\n\nwhich\n\ntemperatures\n\ncovers\nand\n\nof the provisional\nlimits for other exposure\nperiods\nare\nby the NAS/NRC.\nThe\nrationale\nbehind\nthese provisional\ncompounds\nis presented\nin detail in Reference\n(136) and\nbehind\nthe approach\nis discussed\non pages\n13-1 through\nIn brief,\n\nthe provisional\n\nlong-term\n\nlimits\n\nrecommended\n\nexposures\n\npressures.\n\nfor\nSome\n\nthose\nrecommended\nlimits for specific\ngeneral\nphilosophy\n13-4.\nwere\n\nchosen\n\nwith\n\nthe objective\nof avoiding:\nadverse\nhealth\neffects,\neither immediate\nor delayed;\ndegradation\nof performance;\nand interference\nwith physiological\nstudies\non\ncrew\nmembers.\nThe provisional\n60-minute\nemergency\nlimits\nare designed\nto\navoid\nsignificant\ndegradation\nin crew\nperformance\nin emergencies\nand to avoid\npermanent\nhealth\ninjury.\nThey\ncontain\nessentially\nno safety factor,\nand\ntransitory\neffects may\nresult.\nBecause\nof the inadequacies\nof the data mentioned\nabove,\nparticularly\nthe fact that most\ncurrent\ntoxicologic\ndata are based\non non-continuous\nexposure,\nand because\nof uncertainty\nas to synergism\namong\nchemicals,\nand to allow for the possibility\nof minor\nexcursions\nabove\nthe\nceiling limit,\na safety factor has been\napplied\nto each 90 and 1000-day\nlimit\nvalue.\nThe\nmagnitude\nof the safety factor differs according\nto the toxicologic\ncategory\nof the contaminant.\nIf the contaminant\nis an irritant at the threshold\nof response,\nan estimated\nfactor\nof 5 is included\nin the limit.\nIf the contaminan\nis capable\nof producing\nsystemic,\nirreversible\ninjury,\na factor\nof 20 is\nincluded.\nThe\nduration\nof exposure\nto which\na limit value applies\nis determined\nby\nthe type of response\ninduced\nby a given contaminant.\nIf a local irritant (e.g.,\nthe butanones},\nthe Committee\nfelt that so long as the concentration\nwas\nkept\nbelow\nthe irritant level no cumulative\neffects would\noccur.\nIn such cases,\nthe 90-day\nlimit applies\nequally\nto a 1000-day\nmission.\nWhen,\nhowever,\nthe\ncontaminant\nhas the potential\nfor cumulative\naction,\nalbeit at an exposure\nlevel well above\nthe provisional\nlimit for 90 days,\na reduction\nappropriate\nto\nthe seriousness\nof the response\nis made\nfor the 1000-day\nmission.\nIn such\ninstances,\na five-fold\nreduction\nin the 90-day\nlimit has been\narbitrarily\nmade\n(e.g.,\nchloroform,\ndioxane}.\nThe\nother non-TLV\nlimits\nare those previously\nrecommended\nfor nuclear\nsubmarine\nexposures\nby the NAS-NRC\nCommittee\non The predicted\ndeposition\nin the respiratory\npassages\nthat in space,\nas on Earth,\nthe nose\nor\n\ncharacteristics\nof particle\nfor the weightless\nenvironmouth\nshould\ncontinue\nto\n\noperate\nas highly\nefficient filters, protecting\nthe lower\nrespiratory\npassages\nfrom\nall particles\nand droplets\nabove\nabout\n10 microns\nin respect,\nit should\nbe pointed\nout that the use of powdered\nchemicals\nof particle\nsizes greater\nthan 10 microns\nin space\nwould\nbe an\nimportant\nsafety measure.\nIt is possible\nfor an astronaut\nto be exposed\nto aerosols\nand droplets\n(e.g.,\nliquid ejected\nas a fine spray)\nless than about\n10 microns\nin diameter.\nThe\n"deposition\ncurves"\npredict\nthat fewer\ninhaled\nparticles\nand droplets\nbetween\nabout\n0.5 and about\nI0 microns\nin diameter\nwill be deposited\nin the\nlower\nrespiratory\npassages,\nespecially\nin the pulmonary\nregion,\n(Figure\niZ-Z3a\nvs. 12-23b)\nin the weightless\nas compared\nto the one-gravity\nenvironment.\nThis implies\nthat weightlessness\nmight\noffer some\nprotection\nto an\nastronaut\nfrom\ncertain\ncontaminants\nwhich,\nif inhaled\nin a similar\nconcentration in a unit gravity\n\n13 -88\n\nenvironment,\n\nwould\n\nbe\n\nirritating\n\nto or\n\ndamage\n\nalveoli\n\n/and respiratory\nbronchioles,\nor produce\nsystemic\ntoxic effects by being\nabsorbed.\nIt is of interest\nto note that weightlessness\nexerts\nits greatest\nprotective\neffect in the pulmonary\nor non-ciliated\nregion\nof the respiratory\npassages\n-- a region\nwhere\ndeposited\ncontaminants\nare not moved\nout of the\nrespiratory\npassages\nby ciliary action.\nThe\nzero-gravity\ndeposition\npatterns\nimply\nthat the concentration\nof particles\nand droplets\none micron\nin diameter\ninhaled\ninto the respiratory\npassages\nin the weightless\nenvironment\ncould be\napproximately\ndoubled\nbefore\nthe percent\ndeposition\nof such contaminants\nin\nthe pulmonary\nregion\nin this environment\nwould\nbe equivalent\nto their percent\ndeposition\nin the unit gravity\nenvironment.\nSimilarly,\nthe inhaled\nconcentration of particles\nand droplets\ncould be increased\nby approximately\n6 times\nfor particles\nand droplets\n2 microns\nin diameter,\n7 times\nfor those\n3, 4, and\n5 microns\nin diameter,\n6 times\nfor those 6 microns\nin diameter,\n5 times\nfor\nthose\n7 and 8 microns\nin diameter,\nand 3 times\nfor those\n9 microns\nin\ndiameter.\nHowever,\neven though\nit is predicted\nthat the pulmonary\ndeposition\nof inhaled\nparticles\nand droplets\nbetween\nabout\n0.5 and about\nl0 microns\nin\ndiameter\nwill be significantly\nreduced\nin the weightless\nenvironment,\none\nmust\nremember\nthat such\ncontaminating\nparticles\nor droplets\ncould\nstill be\nsuspended\nina concentration\nwhich\nwould\nbe harmful.\nSince the weightless\nspace-cabin\nenvironment\npercent\ndeposition\nof particles\nand droplets\nbelow\n\ndoes\nabout\n\nnot alter the high\n0.5 microns\nin\n\ndiameter\nin the lower\nrespiratory\npassages,\nthe consequences\nof inhaling\nsuch contaminants\nwill probably\nnot be different\nas compared\nto the onegravity\nsea-level\nair environment.\nContaminants\nof this size are most\nlikely\nto be in the form\nof fumes\nor smoke.\nSince particles\nor droplets\nbelow\n0.9\nmicrons\nin diameter\nwill apparently\nnot be deposited\nin the nasal\n(or oral)\nregions\nof the respiratory\npassages,\ntheir inhalation\nshould\nnot produce\nclinical problems\nin the upper\nrespiratory\npassages.\nOn the other hand,\nbecause\nof the very high percent\ndeposition\nof particles\nand droplets\nbelow\nabout\n0.5 microns\nin diameter,\ntracheobronchial\nand pulmonary\ntissues\ncould\n\nbe\n\nselectively\n\nirritated\n\nby\n\nparticles\n\nof this\n\nsize.\n\nWhether\nor not particles\nor droplets\nlarger\nthan a few hundred\nmicrons\nin diameter\n(e.g.,\nseveral\nhundred\nmicrons\nto I cm)\ncan be inhaled\nwill\ndepend\nless on particle\nand droplet\nsize, and more\nand more\non such important factors\nas particle\nor droplet\nshape\nand density,\ntheir spatial relationship\nto the inspiratory\nair streamand\nmouth\nand nasal openings,\ntheir velocities\nand directions\nof movement\nrelative to an astronaut,\nthe velocity-time\nprofiles\nof the inspiratory\nand expiratory\nair streams,\nand the duration\nof the pause\nbetween\ninspiration\nand expiration\n(Z2).\nTaking\nall of these factors\ninto\nconsideration,\nit is predicted\nthat various\nparticles,\nespecially\nthose\nof low\ndensity,\nand droplets\nof possibly\nup to 1 cm\nin diameter,\ncould very well be\ninhaled\nin the weightless\nenvironment.\nAccordingly,\nit is thought\nthat as\ncompared\nto on Earth,\nan astronaut\nin space\nmight\nrun a somewhat\nhigher\nrisk not only of inhaling\nlarge particles\nand droplets\ninto his nose and mouth,\nbut also of aspirating\nlarge particles\nand droplets\nof up to 1 cm\nin diameter\ninto his lower\nrespiratory\ntract.\nThe medical\nsignificance\nand treatment\nof\nemergencies\nto the respiratory\ntract,\nskin and eye from\nparticulates\nin\nspace\ncabins\nhas Other biological effects such as on tracheal\ncilia and lower biological forms have also been reported.\nThe concentration\nof aerosol ions in the natural or submarine atmospheres has always been low.\nIn nuclear submarines, sparking electrical equipment and radium dials were\nprobably responsible for ion concentrations of less than 1000/cm 3 averaging\nabout 450 (+) ions and 250 (-) ions/cm 3 (102). No data have been obtained in\noperating\nspace\ncabins.\nIn view\nof the low concentration\nof aerosol\nions in\nsubmarines\n, the\nuncertain\nsignificance\nof the experiments\nwith isolated\ntracheal\npreparations,\nand equivocal\nresults\nof studies\nwith human\nbehavior\n( 9,\n105,\nI06, 140) the potential\nsignificance\nof these aerosols\nin space\ncabins\nis not
